    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="2ea9c4c955f243468febd11a1c2a4a1e">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'fc3bdb06-c9c9-4d7b-91b2-ffac278bba85'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d5227722" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>As anode for sodium-ion battery, a multi-phase composite was designed and fabricated via electrospinning method through heating at different carbonization temperature. The fabricated composite was composed of Sb and Sn nanodomains that embedded in the porous carbon nanofibers which containing Sb<sub>2</sub>SnO<sub>5</sub> nanoparticles encapsulated inner, which exhibits the varied amount of Sb/Sn nanodomains and the effect on the electrochemical performance, especially, the Sn/Sb@SSO@PCFs-600 shows unexpected high capacity at current 3.0Ag<sup>1</sup> and the retention ratio of 80% after 200cycles.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b0aa52c5-648e-423a-ba34-0b83d6f314a0/slct202302417-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b0aa52c5-648e-423a-ba34-0b83d6f314a0/slct202302417-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0b069187-bcd8-4d12-aeb7-29d0994238fe/slct202302417-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b0aa52c5-648e-423a-ba34-0b83d6f314a0/slct202302417-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d5227725" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Embedding the metal phase and modifying the microstructure is an important way to improve the storage capability of Sb<sub>2</sub>SnO<sub>5</sub> (SSO) anodes. Pertaining to sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), we design and fabricate Sb and Sn embedding porous carbon wires (PCWs) with SSO encapsulated (SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs) composite as a high capability anode in this study. Not only accelerate the Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion, but also augment the stress resistance and the electrochemical reaction reversibility for the multiphase composite. Furthermore, the porous structure finely increases the ions diffusion kinetics, preventing the aggregation of Sn, Sb, and SSO particles, which achieves high reversible capacity while eliminating structure damage. As a consequence, the fabricated SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs electrode exhibited exceptional cycling stability, maintaining a high reversible capacity of 448mAhg<sup>1</sup> after 150 charge/discharge cycles, and good rate capability with current density up to 5.0Ag<sup>1</sup>. This new composite material studied in current work could shed light for the creation of sodium ion batteries with high specific energies in the near future.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="slct202302417-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="slct202302417-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) has attracted great interest, especially in the field of large-scale energy storage.<span><sup><a href="#slct202302417-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0001_R_d5227714e424" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Electrochem. Commun.">1</a>-<a href="#slct202302417-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0003_R_d5227714e424" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nano Energy">3</a></sup></span> The larger ionic radius (1.02) of Na<sup>+</sup> compared to that of Li<sup>+</sup> (0.76), as well as the sluggish diffusion kinetics and enormous volume variation of active materials during the charging/discharging process are the main challenges faced by the SIBs, which stimulating to find suitable electrode materials with high capacity and prolonged life.<span><sup><a href="#slct202302417-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0004_R_d5227714e431" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Electrochim. Acta.">4</a>-<a href="#slct202302417-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0006_R_d5227714e431" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Power Sources">6</a></sup></span> So far, the property of Na<sup>+</sup> ions storage has been studied in varied substances, such as the carbon-based materials,<span><sup><a href="#slct202302417-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0007_R_d5227714e437" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - J. Alloys Compd.">7</a>-<a href="#slct202302417-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0009_R_d5227714e437" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nano Lett.">9</a></sup></span> alloy-based composite,<span><sup><a href="#slct202302417-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0010_R_d5227714e440" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">10</a>-<a href="#slct202302417-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0014_R_d5227714e440" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nat. Nanotechnology.">14</a></sup></span> and metal oxides,<span><sup><a href="#slct202302417-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0015_R_d5227714e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Engl.">15</a>-<a href="#slct202302417-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0017_R_d5227714e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ChemSusChem">17</a></sup></span> etal. Of which, the metal oxide Sb<sub>2</sub>SnO<sub>5</sub> has a considerable theoretical capacity of 590.8mAhg<sup>1</sup> and low cost that shows a great potential prospect. However, the poor conductivity and high Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion barrier, as well as rapid capacity attenuation, make its application restricted. Various tactics have been used to resolve the aforementioned problems, such as compressing materials to the nanoscale,<span><sup><a href="#slct202302417-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0018_R_d5227714e455" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Electrochim. Acta">18</a>-<a href="#slct202302417-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0021_R_d5227714e455" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nano Lett.">21</a></sup></span> building carbon composites,<span><sup><a href="#slct202302417-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0022_R_d5227714e458" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nano Energy">22</a>-<a href="#slct202302417-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0026_R_d5227714e458" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Mater. Chem. A.">26</a></sup></span> and introducing the multi-phase structure.<span><sup><a href="#slct202302417-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0027_R_d5227714e461" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Int. J. Hydrogen Energy">27</a>-<a href="#slct202302417-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0029_R_d5227714e461" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">29</a></sup></span> In previous studies,<span><sup><a href="#slct202302417-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0030_R_d5227714e464" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Alloys Compd.">30</a></sup></span> we compressed SSO into nanofibers with a porous structure, which not only buffered the volume change in the sodification/desodification process but also provided more reaction active sites. Consequently, the electrochemical property is obviously improved. In addition, through the carbonization process, the Sn and Sb nanodomains are generated insitu around the SSO, which reduces the diffusion barrier of Na<sup>+</sup> ions during the process of charging and discharging, and improves the specific capacity of the fabricated composite electrode. Here, adding the metal phase to the oxides is an important mean to improve the alloying reaction. But the specific amount of Sb/Sn nanodomains regulated by temperature and their effect on electrochemical performance of the fabricated composite has not been studied further and systematically. Therefore, in the current work, the composite SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs fabricated via different heat treat temperature was further studied. And the prepared composite has the following advantages: 1)The isolated SSO, Sn and Sb particles are interconnected through the carbon wires to establish a fast electron diffusion channel and increase the Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion coefficient; 2)The increase of Sn and Sb phases not only helps to enhance the Na<sup>+</sup> ions store ability of the entire electrode but also enlarges the contacting area between the metal and its oxides, which facilitate the transfer dynamic of the composite material. 3)The phase adjustment effect makes SSO have better ductility, which reduce the internal stress of volume change of SSO and making better reversibility. 4)The porous carbon wire acts as a shield for SSO, avoiding the direct contact with electrolyte, expect depressing the large volume expansion of Sn and Sb. Consequently, the embedding metal phase and adjusting the microstructure of compounds is an important means for improving the cycling stabilization of SSO electrode and optimize its electrochemical performance.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="slct202302417-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="slct202302417-sec-0002-title"> Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <p><b>Synthesis of the SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs</b>: In an electrostatic spinning process, commercial SSO (Sb<sub>2</sub>SnO<sub>5</sub>), PVP (polyvinylpyrrolidone), and PMMA (polymethyl methacrylate) which are purchased from Aladdin, were used as basic materials to construct porous nanowires. A high-energy ball mill was first used to grind the SSO particle for ten hours. Parallel, the PMMA and PVP dissolved in N, N-Dimethylformamide (DMF)and produced a clear liquid mixed with a magnetic stirrer for 24hours. Then, the nanoparticles of ground SSO were then ultrasonically disseminated in the obtained solution. The electric field of 20kV were used to carry out the electrospinning. The polymer solution was sprayed onto the aluminum plate with a syringe pump at a speed of 0.2mm per minute, 10cm away from the needle tip. The produced electrospun membranes was heated to the desired temperature using the following process after being dried overnight in a 50C oven. The electro-spun membranes was first stabilized in an argon-hydrogen mixture at 100C for one hour before being heated to 300C to stabilize the wire morphology. Lastly, the carbonization process was conducted under the protection by Ar/H<sub>2</sub> (5%) gas at 550~700C and maintained for two hours.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="slct202302417-sec-0003">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="slct202302417-sec-0003-title"> Materials characterization</h3>
            
            <p>The transmission electron microscope (TEM/HRTEM) based on a JEOL JEM-2100 equipped with a charge-coupled device (CCD) camera and running at an acceleration voltage of 200kV was used to determine the SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs morphology. The crystalline phase of the SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs was investigated using an X'Pert PRO diffractometer (XRD, Shimadzu, and measurement angles between 10 and 80 at a scan rate of 10 per minute). Additionally, the chemical makeup and bonding condition of the synthesized materials were further examined using X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, Escalb 250xi).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="slct202302417-sec-0004">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="slct202302417-sec-0004-title"> Electrochemical Measurement</h3>
            
            <p>To evaluate the electrochemical performance of the synthesized SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs anodes for SIBs, CR2032 coin cells were constructed using the Whatman GF/G glass membrane as the separator and the sodium foil as the counter electrode. The working electrode was consisted of 70% active materials (SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-600), 15% super P (carbon black), and 15% PVDF. In addition, the electrolyte was a solution of NaPF<sub>6</sub> (1.0M) in dimethyl ether (DME) (100%) solvent. The active materials were produced in loading mass ranging from 0.70 to 1.50mgcm<sup>2</sup> after the slurry was deposited on a copper foil and desiccated at 50C for 10hours. Assemble the coin cells using the accomplished anodes in a glovebox (O<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O&lt;0.5ppm), and then left aside for 8hours before the subsequent electrochemical test. On a DH7001 electrochemical workstation, the cyclic voltammetry (CV) was examined in various scan rates and the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measured in 1M~0.1Hz frequency. On the Landt and Neware testing systems, all charge/discharge cycles were performed. The discharge cutoff voltage was set to 0.01V, and the charge cutoff voltage was 1.5V. The specific capacity was calculated on the basis of the mass of the active materials</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="slct202302417-sec-0005">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="slct202302417-sec-0005-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The schematic diagram of the fabrication process of the SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs porous nanowires is shown in Figure<a href="#slct202302417-fig-0001">1</a>. The SSO particle inherits the structural characteristics of its predecessor even after ball milling. The relatively small particle size of the SSO not only makes for good dispersion in the PMMA/PVP solution, but also helps the homodisperse of SSO in the nanowires. In the carbonization process, the amount of in-situ formation of Sn/Sb phase can be controlled by the carbonization temperature. More information about the TEM images of SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs composites is also provided in FigureS1ab. As indicated, the SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-600 are mainly composed of the nanowires with the size of 50~70nm in diameter and smaller nanoparticles dispersed inner. Figure<a href="#slct202302417-fig-0002">2</a> is the HRTEM images of the SSO experiencing different carbonization temperature, as shown, the size of the Sn and Sb nanodomain varied companying with the temperature change in the range of 550C, 600C, 650C and 700C. It is observed clearly that the Sb and Sn nanodomains are around the SSO matrix in close contact, and the amount as well as the size of Sn (211), Sb (012) and Sb (110) nanodomains gradually increased with the change of temperature from 550C, 600C to 650C, but as the temperature rose to 700C, the number of nanodomains did not further increase significantly, but the size of nanodomains really increased. It is clearly observed that the diameter size of Sn and Sb domains is about 515nm, 1025nm and 2040nm for the SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-600, SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-650, and SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-700 samples, respectively. Correspondingly, the lattice spacing of the places of Sn (101) and Sn (211) are 0.28nm and 0.20nm, separately, while the Sb (012) and Sb (110) planes correspond to the lattice spacing of 0.31nm and 0.21nm, respectively. Additionally, it should be noted that the particle boundary and crystal surface play an important role in the sodium ion storage process: Firstly, the conductivity of ions at the interface is high, and more transfer channels are provided for sodium ion transfer.<span><sup><a href="#slct202302417-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0031_R_d5227714e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Mater. Inter.">31</a></sup></span> Secondly, grain boundaries can be used as charge storage tank, which can store more Na<sup>+</sup> ions.<span><sup><a href="#slct202302417-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0032_R_d5227714e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">32</a>, <a href="#slct202302417-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0033_R_d5227714e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Nano Energy">33</a></sup></span> Apart from these, it is should be noted that the interface ratio per unit area is also critical to contribute the specific capacity of the whole electrode. What's more, the difference of Fermi energy level between Sn, Sb and SSO also facilitate the electrons transfer and the improvement of the electrochemical kinetics of the fabricated anodes.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="slct202302417-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9e000768-0982-413e-9d46-246ef92fd8d5/slct202302417-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>

               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Meanwhile, the crystal structure of SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs was further verified by XRD patterns corresponding to the phases from Sn, Sb and SSO. From Figure<a href="#slct202302417-fig-0003">3</a>, it is visible that the XRD diffraction peaks are sharp and strong, indicating that the synthesized material has good crystallinity. As shown in Figure<a href="#slct202302417-fig-0003">3a</a>, the peaks centered at 29 and 31 correspond to the Sn (101), and Sn (211) diffraction planes (PDF#04-0673), respectively, while the peak at 28.6 correlates to the Sb (110) plane (PDF#35-0732). In addition, the peaks at 26.6, 33.9, 37.9 correspond to the SnO<sub>2</sub> (PDF# 411445) (110), (101), and (200) planes, respectively. Specially, with the increase of heating handle temperature, the diffraction peak intensity of Sb and Sn gradually increased, while peaks intensity corresponding to the SSO gradually decreased, in addition, no significant peaks for other phase or impurity were detected. Additionally, combing the XRD pattern with the HRTEM images, it can be deduced that nano-size Sb phase was first reduced insitu around the SSO matrix and then the Sn phase, forming the Sn/Sb/SSO composite structure, accompanying with the increased number of grain boundaries and interfaces. As mentioned, these boundaries provide the interesting character of not only providing more active sites for Na<sup>+</sup> ions storage but also enhancing the fast charge transfer. In addition, the XRD patterns for the disassembled cells after several hundred charge/discharge test is also provided in supporting FigureS2. It was found that the peaks from Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> at 19.1, 60.6, and the Na<sub>3</sub>Sb peaks at 21.3, 33.5, 34.3 and 66.2 appeared in the cycled electrode, except the peaks from Cu collector.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="slct202302417-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/69424e1e-ffec-413b-8a39-89c9e50d1c05/slct202302417-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/69424e1e-ffec-413b-8a39-89c9e50d1c05/slct202302417-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/acbba37c-f2c5-46c6-8499-c0925519f74c/slct202302417-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/69424e1e-ffec-413b-8a39-89c9e50d1c05/slct202302417-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=slct202302417-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fslct.202302417" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(ac) XRD patterns of SSO, SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-550, SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-600, SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-650 and SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-700 at (a) 10~80, (b)20~40, and (c)40~50.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>XPS test is also utilized in order to further verify the chemical state evolution of composite at different temperature. As shown in the full XPS spectra in Figure<a href="#slct202302417-fig-0004">4a</a> for the compared samples, Sn, Sb, C and O elements can be clearly observed. The C1s signals in supporting FigureS3 for the four compared samples are nearly same, including C=O (288.18eV), COSb (286.38eV) and CC (284.88eV). The intensity of O1s at 528.5eV gradually increases as carbonization temperature increased as shown in Figure<a href="#slct202302417-fig-0004">4b</a>, indicating the electrons transfer from O<sup>2</sup> and the formation of O<sup>o</sup>. More, the Sn3d<sub>5/2</sub> and Sn3d<sub>3/2</sub> peaks for the Sn<sup>4+</sup> are located at 486.8eV and 495.3eV, respectively, and shown in Figure<a href="#slct202302417-fig-0004">4c</a>.<span><sup><a href="#slct202302417-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0034_R_d5227714e648" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Electrochim. Acta">34</a>, <a href="#slct202302417-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0035_R_d5227714e648" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Energy Mater.">35</a></sup></span> Moreover, the Sb3d<sub>5/2</sub> and Sb3d<sub>3/2</sub> peaks are located at 530.9eV and 539.9eV, separately (Figure<a href="#slct202302417-fig-0004">4d</a>).<span><sup><a href="#slct202302417-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0036_R_d5227714e659" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - ChemElectroChem">36</a></sup></span> It should be noted that the intensity of Sn<sup>0</sup>3d<sub>5/2</sub> at 485.3eV and Sn<sup>0</sup>3d<sub>3/2</sub> at 493.8eV also gradually increased with the increasing carbonization temperature. Similarly, the peak intensity of the Sb<sup>0</sup>3d<sub>5/2</sub> at 528.6eV and Sb<sup>0</sup>3d<sub>3/2</sub> at 537.8eV also showed the raised signal accompanying with the elevated carbonization temperature.<span><sup><a href="#slct202302417-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0037_R_d5227714e679" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Alloys Compd.">37</a></sup></span> This indicates that when the heating temperature was raised, the proportion of Sn and Sb to the SSO exhibited the similar evolution rule. The higher corresponds to the more. More detailed explanation of the composites constituent evolution is also provided in Table<a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#slct202302417-tbl-0001">1</a> below, as shown, the relative proportions of Sn, Sb and SSO in the composite is list in the table and shown as following:
</p>

         <div class="paragraph-element">To further investigate the electrochemical performance of the as-obtained SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs anodes for SIBs, the CV profiles for the initial three cycles measured at a scan rate of 0.1mVs<sup>1</sup> for the SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-600 sample is depicted in Figure<a href="#slct202302417-fig-0005">5a</a>. It is obvious that, during the cathodic scan initially, a clear wide peak appeared at 0.49V was visible, which was caused by the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions and the conversion of SSO into Sn and Sb. As the charge progressed, the formation of Na<sub>3</sub>Sb and Na<sub>3.75</sub>Sn alloys is responsible for the other two peaks at 0.33V and 0.02V, respectively. Meanwhile, in the subsequent anodic scans, a broad oxidation band centered at 0.08V corresponded to the Sn and Sb to SSO phase conversion reaction. And the high peaks at 0.56V and 0.22V were attributed to the step-by-step dealloying of Na<sub>3.75</sub>Sn, while the peak at 0.78V was attributed to the dealloying of Na<sub>3</sub>Sb.<span><sup><a href="#slct202302417-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0037_R_d5227714e817" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Alloys Compd.">37</a>-<a href="#slct202302417-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0039_R_d5227714e817" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Nanoscale">39</a></sup></span> Notably, the CV curves in the original cycles nearly coincided, which indicates that the SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-600 anode has better reversible charging/discharging process, being responsible for the ensured cycling stability. More information for the CV compare is also provided in FigureS4. In addition, the reversible reaction mechanism of SSO, Sn and Sb with Na<sup>+</sup> ions to generate Na<sub>3.75</sub>Sn, Na<sub>3</sub>Sb and Na<sub>2</sub>O during the charge/discharge procedure can be expressed in the following Equation<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#slct202302417-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>:
            <div class="inline-equation" id="slct202302417-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/2f4c92bc-9792-4140-81f9-6a804bf9978e/slct202302417-math-0001.png"></span><mjx-container sre-explorer-id="0" tabindex="0" role="application" class="CtxtMenu_Attached_0" ctxtmenu_oldtabindex="1" ctxtmenu_counter="0" style="position: relative;"><mjx-lazy data-mjx-lazy="0" aria-hidden="true"></mjx-lazy><mjx-assistive-mml unselectable="on" display="inline"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" data-semantic-type="empty" data-semantic-role="unknown" data-semantic-="" data-semantic-speech=""></math></mjx-assistive-mml></mjx-container>
                  </span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
            </div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="slct202302417-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/41f9ec49-d0fd-47e5-8396-a666d973c9a4/slct202302417-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/41f9ec49-d0fd-47e5-8396-a666d973c9a4/slct202302417-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/90fabdcd-4f8d-46ed-9dd9-ca1c5b6575f6/slct202302417-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/41f9ec49-d0fd-47e5-8396-a666d973c9a4/slct202302417-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=slct202302417-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fslct.202302417" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-600 electrode CV curves at 0.1mVs<sup>1</sup> scan rates; (b)Compare of charge and discharge of the SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-600 sample in the 3<sup>rd</sup>, 20<sup>th</sup>, 100<sup>th</sup> and 150<sup>th</sup>cycles; (c)Compare of the cycling property of the samples at 50mAhg<sup>1</sup> current density; (d)Rate performance compare of the SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-600 at 50, 100, 300, 500, and 800mAg<sup>1</sup>, and (e)Compare of the cycling properties of different samples at 3Ag<sup>1</sup>.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Figure<a href="#slct202302417-fig-0005">5b</a> further displays the galvanostatic charge/discharge voltage plot for the SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-600 electrode at various cycles. It is evident that the charge and discharge curves overlap well even after 150cycles, indicating good reversibility and capacity retention capability. Figure<a href="#slct202302417-fig-0005">5c</a> compares of cycling property of the different samples at the same 50mAg<sup>1</sup> current density. As shown, the compared electrodes demonstrated remarkable cycling stability except the initial capacity decline, which maintains a high specific capacity of 447.8mAhg<sup>1</sup>, without discernible capacity degradation after being charged and discharged 150 times. Moreover, the initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) of the SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-600 electrode is 59.3%, and after the original three cycles, the CE progressively rose to almost 100% and stayed constant in the subsequent cycles. By contrast, the SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-650 and SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-700 display the inferior capacity retention and cycling stability, and the specific capacity remains continuous decay during charging/discharging process, after 100cycles only 294.0 and 214.9mAhg<sup>1</sup> discharge capacity was hold for SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-650 and SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-700, respectively. Additionally, according to rate performance testing shown in Figure<a href="#slct202302417-fig-0005">5d</a>, it is observed that the SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-600 electrode shows the best capacity delivering, which renders 570.2, 522.7, 464.7, 426.8 and 388.2mAhg<sup>1</sup> at corresponding current densities of 50, 100, 300, 500, and 800mAg<sup>1</sup>. From the electrochemical testing results, it can observed that the rate capability of the current SSO@C composite is also close to the previous reported SnSe-based oxides.<span><sup><a href="#slct202302417-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0040_R_d5227714e964" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - ACS. Appl. Mater. Inters.">40</a></sup></span> Meanwhile, as exhibited in Figure<a href="#slct202302417-fig-0005">5e</a>, the compared electrodes are cycled at 3.0Ag<sup>1</sup> current density, the specific capacity of Sn/Sb@SSO@PCFs-600 can sustain 427.5mAhg<sup>1</sup>, and a capacity retention rate of up to 80% even after 200cycles, which was significantly higher than the others. In the supporting FigureS4d, the SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-600 also exhibited specific capacities of 380.8, 356.0, 339.2, 325.5, and 319.4mAhg<sup>1</sup> at current densities of 1, 2, 3, 4, and 5Ag<sup>1</sup>, separately. The better electrochemical performance of the SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-600 can be attributed to the following traits: 1)The appropriate ratio of Sn and Sb, to SSO, and the applicable crystal boundaries and domain size, which increase the composites specific capacity and charge transfer channels; 2)The porous and nanowire structure give enough space to accommodate the considerable volume expansion upon Na<sup>+</sup> ions insertion.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">To further analyze the charge transfer kinetics of the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions in the different fabricated electrodes. The CV testing combined with the curves fitting at different scanning rates was also performed and analyzed. As can be seen in Figure<a href="#slct202302417-fig-0006">6a</a>, a series of CV curves tested in the voltage window range of 0.001~1.5V with the scanning speed set between 0.1 and 0.5mVs<sup>1</sup>, exhibit high and wide peaks as the scanning rates rise during the process of sodiation and desodiation. More important, the diffusion coefficient for Na<sup>+</sup> in the fabricated electrode can be evaluated base on the Randles-Sevcik formula as stated in Equation(2) via the peak current I<sub>max</sub> and scan rate <sup>1/2</sup>, which is estimated by&nbsp;the value of the slope from the fitting of straight line.<span><sup><a href="#slct202302417-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0041_R_d5227714e998" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - J. Alloys Compd.">41</a></sup></span><div class="inline-equation" id="slct202302417-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/1322c7dc-f5fc-478b-a8ce-448b51b991f9/slct202302417-math-0002.png"></span><mjx-container sre-explorer-id="1" tabindex="0" role="application" class="CtxtMenu_Attached_0" ctxtmenu_oldtabindex="1" ctxtmenu_counter="1" style="position: relative;"><mjx-lazy data-mjx-lazy="1" aria-hidden="true"></mjx-lazy><mjx-assistive-mml unselectable="on" display="inline"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" data-semantic-type="empty" data-semantic-role="unknown" data-semantic-="" data-semantic-speech=""></math></mjx-assistive-mml></mjx-container>
                  </span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
            </div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="slct202302417-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a41a75c8-8af0-45bf-822b-a78ab2172491/slct202302417-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a41a75c8-8af0-45bf-822b-a78ab2172491/slct202302417-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/163f7c7b-9966-4d88-886b-292f734fb63a/slct202302417-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a41a75c8-8af0-45bf-822b-a78ab2172491/slct202302417-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=slct202302417-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fslct.202302417" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-600 anode CV curves at various scan rates: 0.1, 0.2, 0.3, 0.4, and 0.5mvs<sup>1</sup>. (b)Fitting results between the peak current and scanning rate. (c)Compare of the EIS for various samples. (d)Plotting of the Zre and <sup>1/2</sup> within the frequency range of 0.1 to 1.0Hz. (e)GITT curves during the first cycle, and (f)GITT plots of the corresponding log (D<sub>Na+</sub>) of SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-600.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">where n, A, Do<sub>x</sub>, C, and  represents the number of electrons per molecule, the electrode area, the diffusion coefficient, the content of starting material, and the scan rate for the CV measurement, respectively. The diffusion coefficient of the various electrode materials can be qualitatively evaluated and plotted in Figure<a href="#slct202302417-fig-0006">6b</a>, in which, the value of the slope for the fitting lines is 0.29, 0.35, 0.09, and 0.22 for the SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-550, SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-600, SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-650, and SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-700, respectively. It is clear that the SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-600 has the biggest slope value and the highest confusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> ions among the compared electrodes. And more information is also feasible in supporting FigureS5. Further, the EIS and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was also utilized to reveal the diffusion kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> ions and the electron transfer capacity in the composite material. Generally, for the EIS plots, in the high-to-medium frequency range of the Nyquist diagram, the charge transfer resistance through the interface, such solid electrolyte interface (SEI), is depicted by a semicircle, and the charge transfer resistance in the bulk material is represented by a straight line in the low frequency range.<span><sup><a href="#slct202302417-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0042_R_d5227714e1095" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - ACS Appl. Nano Mater.">42</a>, <a href="#slct202302417-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0043_R_d5227714e1095" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Appl. Catal. B">43</a></sup></span> According to the EIS measurement results shown in Figure<a href="#slct202302417-fig-0006">6c</a>, the semicircle diameter of the SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-600 is clearly smaller than the others, indicating a reduced charge transfer resistance and improved kinetics of charge. The inset is the equivalent circuit, in which R<sub>e</sub>, R<sub>SEI</sub>, and R<sub>ct</sub>, stands for the resistance of the electrical contact, and the Na<sup>+</sup> ions flowing through the SEI layer, as well as the charge transfer resistance, respectively, and the W represented the Warburg impedance related to Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion in the active materials, while C and Q represent the double layer capacitance and constant phase components, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#slct202302417-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0044_R_d5227714e1113" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Nanoscale">44</a>, <a href="#slct202302417-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0045_R_d5227714e1113" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Nano Energy">45</a></sup></span> The following Equations(3) and (4) can be used to obtain the Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion coefficient (D<sub>Na+</sub>) using the slope of the Warburg plots:<span><sup><a href="#slct202302417-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0046_R_d5227714e1120" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Electrochim. Acta">46</a>, <a href="#slct202302417-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0047_R_d5227714e1120" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - J. Power Sources">47</a></sup></span><div class="inline-equation" id="slct202302417-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/116e9e8f-bc28-4a1c-81ac-3de745ff9abd/slct202302417-math-0003.png"></span><mjx-container sre-explorer-id="2" tabindex="0" role="application" class="CtxtMenu_Attached_0" ctxtmenu_oldtabindex="1" ctxtmenu_counter="2" style="position: relative;"><mjx-lazy data-mjx-lazy="2" aria-hidden="true"></mjx-lazy><mjx-assistive-mml unselectable="on" display="inline"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" data-semantic-type="empty" data-semantic-role="unknown" data-semantic-="" data-semantic-speech=""></math></mjx-assistive-mml></mjx-container>
                  </span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>
            
            <div class="inline-equation" id="slct202302417-disp-0004"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/072a299b-0e83-4166-bcbf-5c9d0d29ec4d/slct202302417-math-0004.png"></span><mjx-container sre-explorer-id="3" tabindex="0" role="application" class="CtxtMenu_Attached_0" ctxtmenu_oldtabindex="1" ctxtmenu_counter="3" style="position: relative;"><mjx-lazy data-mjx-lazy="3" aria-hidden="true"></mjx-lazy><mjx-assistive-mml unselectable="on" display="inline"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" data-semantic-type="empty" data-semantic-role="unknown" data-semantic-="" data-semantic-speech=""></math></mjx-assistive-mml></mjx-container>
                  </span><span class="inline-equation__label">(4)</span></div>
            </div>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">where the slope of Zre vs <sup>1/2</sup> (=2f) plotting can be used to determine the Warburg factor <i>k</i>. It is deduced that the value of D<sub>Na+</sub> is greater through the smaller <i>k</i> value in Equations(3) and (4). As uncovered in Figure<a href="#slct202302417-fig-0006">6d</a>, the slope value for the SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-600 electrode is 0.62, which is much smaller than that of the SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-650 (0.74) and SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-700 electrodes (0.88). It is worth noting that using the formula (4) combined with the slope values from formula (3), the values of the diffusion coefficient D<sub>Na+</sub> can be obtained for different fabricated electrodes, and the concrete data of the D<sub>Na+</sub> is 0.910<sup>10</sup>cm<sup>2</sup>/s, 1.010<sup>10</sup>cm<sup>2</sup>/s, 0.710<sup>10</sup>cm<sup>2</sup>/s, and 0.510<sup>10</sup>cm<sup>2</sup>/s, for the corresponding SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-550, SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-600, SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-650, and SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-700, respectively, which indicates the highest charge diffusion rate in the SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-600. Similarly, the GITT curve for SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-600 during the initial cycle is also plotted in Figure<a href="#slct202302417-fig-0006">6e</a>, according to the following Equation<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#slct202302417-disp-0005" title="Link to equation">5</a>:<span><sup><a href="#slct202302417-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0048_R_d5227714e1290" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Electrochim. Acta">48</a>, <a href="#slct202302417-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="slct202302417-bib-0049_R_d5227714e1290" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Nanoscale">49</a></sup></span><div class="inline-equation" id="slct202302417-disp-0005"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/6c942e9b-bdd5-4364-9558-af0e8156d546/slct202302417-math-0005.png"></span><mjx-container sre-explorer-id="4" tabindex="0" role="application" class="CtxtMenu_Attached_0" ctxtmenu_oldtabindex="1" ctxtmenu_counter="4" style="position: relative;"><mjx-lazy data-mjx-lazy="4" aria-hidden="true"></mjx-lazy><mjx-assistive-mml unselectable="on" display="inline"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" data-semantic-type="empty" data-semantic-role="unknown" data-semantic-="" data-semantic-speech=""></math></mjx-assistive-mml></mjx-container>
                  </span><span class="inline-equation__label">(5)</span></div>
            </div>
         
         <p>where T is the discharge pulse duration, m<sub>B</sub> and M<sub>B</sub> stand for the active materials mass and molar mass, V<sub>M</sub> for the electrode material's molar volume, S for the electrode/electrolyte contact area, and E<sub>S</sub> and E<sub></sub>, for the voltage change brought on by the pulse and the constant current charge and discharge, respectively. According to the plots in Figure<a href="#slct202302417-fig-0006">6f</a>, it is calculated that the value of the diffusion coefficient D<sub>Na+</sub> of Na<sup>+</sup> ions in the SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs-600 in the first several cycles ranged from the 10<sup>9</sup> to 10<sup>13</sup>cm<sup>2</sup>/s, which is also consistent with the results estimated by the EIS measurement.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="slct202302417-sec-0006">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="slct202302417-sec-0006-title"> Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, the different composite of SSO@Sn/Sb-PCWs fabricated at different temperature are investigated. Combing with the CV, EIS and GITT testing and the fitting results, the charge transfer kinetic is further revealed and analyzed. Firstly, the Sn/Sb@SSO@PCFs-600 electrode exhibits the best electrochemical performance, which is attributed to the proper distribution of the metal Sn/Sb phases and good embedding of SSO in the porous carbon wires, which can relieve the expansion/contraction stress. Secondly, combined with the unique structure design, abundant active sites and carrier diffusion channels are feasible, and the rapid and efficient ions diffusion is realized. Thirdly, through the conducting strategy, not only the bounded regions between metal particles and oxide particles are increased, but also the improved specific capacity and charge transfer kinetic through rational materials design are realized. Therefore, the composite anode materials studied in this paper not only offer a fresh approach to enhancing the electrochemical performance of oxides-based electrodes, but also lay the groundwork for the creation of novel sodium ion battery electrode materials with high specific energy.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="slct202302417-sec-0007">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="slct202302417-sec-0007-title"> Supporting Information Summary</h2>
         
         <p>The Supporting Information includes morphology characterization TEM, and microstructure characterization XPS and XRD, as well as electrochemical performance of CV and rate capacity.</p>
         </section>
